# Tag: k-12 (671 entries)

| # | Title | Type | Description |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | [Organizing Instruction and Study to Improve Student Learning](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/1) | framework | Seven recommendations for using learning and study time effectively in subjects requiring content mastery (science, social studies, mathematics): spacing content review, alternating worked examples with practice problems, combining visual and verbal instruction, linking abstract and concrete concepts, strategic use of quizzes, efficient study time allocation, and deep explanatory questioning. |
| 2 | [Using Student Achievement Data to Support Instructional Decision Making](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/12) | framework | Five recommendations for leveraging student data to monitor academic progress and evaluate teaching: establishing schoolwide data systems, fostering a data-informed culture, integrating data review into instructional improvement cycles, teaching students to interpret their own performance data for goal-setting, and implementing district-level evidence infrastructure. |
| 3 | [Improving Mathematical Problem Solving in Grades 4 Through 8](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/16) | framework | Prepared by Mathematica. Five recommendations for teachers, math coaches, and curriculum developers targeting grades 4–8: preparing problems for whole-class instruction, supporting student self-monitoring and reflection, teaching visual representation, exposing students to multiple solution strategies, and helping students recognize mathematical concepts and notation. |
| 4 | [Teaching Math to Young Children](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/18) | framework | Five recommendations for preschool, prekindergarten, and kindergarten educators: teaching number and operations using developmental progression (Moderate Evidence), teaching geometry and patterns developmentally (Minimal Evidence), using progress monitoring aligned to individual child knowledge (Minimal Evidence), teaching mathematical perspectives on everyday situations (Minimal Evidence), and dedicating daily math time integrated throughout instruction (Minimal Evidence). Each recommendation includes implementation steps and solutions for common obstacles. |
| 5 | [Teaching Academic Content and Literacy to English Learners in Elementary and Middle School](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/19) | framework | Four recommendations for elementary and middle school educators: intensive academic vocabulary instruction using varied activities, integration of oral and written English instruction into content areas, structured opportunities for written language development, and small-group interventions for struggling learners. Includes examples aligned to Common Core standards. |
| 6 | [Teaching Strategies for Improving Algebra Knowledge in Middle and High School Students](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/20) | framework | Three recommendations for middle and high school algebra educators: analyzing algebraic reasoning, utilizing structural representations, and selecting alternative problem-solving approaches. Each recommendation includes implementation steps and common roadblocks. |
| 8 | [Assisting Students Struggling with Mathematics: Intervention in the Elementary Grades](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/26) | framework | Six recommendations for grades K–6, all with strong evidence ratings: systematic instruction, mathematical language instruction, use of concrete representations, number line application, word problem instruction, and timed activities to build fluency. Covers both general student populations and children with disabilities. |
| 9 | [Preparing Young Children for School](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/30) | framework | Seven recommendations distilling contemporary early childhood and preschool research to help prepare young children to benefit from educational opportunities in school settings. |
| 10 | [CAST Universal Design for Learning Guidelines 3.0](https://udlguidelines.cast.org) | framework | Organization: CAST. An educational framework "based on scientific insights into how humans learn" designed as "a tool that can be applied in any discipline or domain to ensure that all learners are able to access and participate in meaningful, challenging learning opportunities." Version 3.0 specifically addresses "critical barriers rooted in biases and systems of exclusion for learners with and without disabilities." Three design principles: Engagement, Representation, and Action & Expression. Overarching goal: learner agency that is "purposeful & reflective, resourceful & authentic, strategic & action-oriented." Downloadable PDF available in 20+ languages. Audience: educators, curriculum developers, learning and instructional designers, researchers, parents. |
| 11 | [Recommendation on the Ethics of Artificial Intelligence](https://www.unesco.org/en/articles/recommendation-ethics-artificial-intelligence?hub=195885) | framework | Adopted 2021, published 2022. UNESCO's inaugural global standard on AI ethics, addressed to all 194 UNESCO member states. Establishes that "the protection of human rights and dignity is the cornerstone" of the framework. Covers transparency, fairness, and human oversight of AI systems across Policy Action Areas addressing data governance, environmental concerns, gender equity, education, research, health, and social wellbeing. |
| 12 | [UNESCO AI Competency Framework for Students](https://www.unesco.org/en/articles/what-you-need-know-about-unescos-new-ai-competency-frameworks-students-and-teachers) | framework | Four core competency areas for students (K-12 and higher education): "a human-centred mindset," "ethics of AI," "AI techniques and applications," and "AI system design." Direct PDF available in English, Arabic, French, Portuguese, Spanish, and Vietnamese at unesdoc.unesco.org (access from direct UNESDOC URL was blocked during audit; description sourced from UNESCO article page). |
| 13 | [UNESCO AI Competency Framework for Teachers](https://www.unesco.org/en/articles/what-you-need-know-about-unescos-new-ai-competency-frameworks-students-and-teachers) | framework | Five competency areas for teachers and educators: "a human-centred mindset," "ethics of AI," "AI foundations and applications," "AI pedagogy," and "AI for professional development." Direct PDF available in English, Arabic, French, Portuguese, Spanish, and Vietnamese at unesdoc.unesco.org (access from direct UNESDOC URL was blocked during audit; description sourced from UNESCO article page). |
| 15 | [EMERALDS: Preparation for Success in Algebra](https://achievethecore.org/page/3367/preparation-for-success-in-algebra-exploring-math-education-relationships-by-analyzing-large-data-sets-emeralds) | report | Principal Investigator: David C. Geary (University of Missouri). Institutions: Human-Computer Interaction Institute at Carnegie Mellon University and Educational Testing Service. "One of the largest longitudinal assessments of what mathematics prepares students for success in Algebra" — uses anonymized large-scale data to identify mathematical competencies in earlier grades that form critical foundations for algebraic success, with particular focus on historically underserved student populations. Report hosted at Achieve the Core; full report linked from that page. |
| 16 | [Lenses on Literature — Evidence for ESSA](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/lenses-on-literature/) | report | Evidence review published at Evidence for ESSA (Johns Hopkins University). Carnegie Learning's Lenses on Literature (formerly Literacy Design Collaborative) earned a "Promising" (Tier 3) ESSA evidence rating. Instructional framework emphasizing daily, standards-driven reading, writing, and discourse organized around disciplinary literacy with complex texts. Primary study: 18,203 students across Kentucky and Pennsylvania (2012–2013). Effect size: +0.05 average; Kentucky: +0.06 (statistically significant); Pennsylvania: +0.03 (non-significant). Includes scaffolds for multilingual learners, embedded assessments using Stanford-aligned rubrics, and professional development with coaching and data analysis. |
| 19 | [Computational Thinking for an Inclusive World](https://digitalpromise.dspacedirect.org/handle/20.500.12265/138) | report | Digital Promise report calling on educators to integrate computational thinking across PreK–12 disciplinary learning with an explicit equity focus, addressing persistent gaps in access to computing education for marginalized students. Argues that CT skills are important for all students—not only future computer science students—and issues two calls to action for designing inclusive computing learning opportunities. |
| 21 | [Assisting Students Struggling with Mathematics: Response to Intervention (RtI) for Elementary and Middle Schools](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/2) | framework | Eight recommendations for elementary and middle school math intervention using the Response to Intervention framework: screen all students and provide targeted support (Moderate), emphasize whole numbers K–5 and rational numbers grades 4–8 (Minimal), use explicit and systematic instruction (Strong), teach problem solving via common structural patterns (Strong), incorporate visual representations (Moderate), build fact fluency with approximately 10 minutes per session (Moderate), monitor student progress throughout intervention (Minimal), and include motivational strategies in secondary and tertiary intervention tiers (Minimal). |
| 22 | [Assisting Students Struggling with Reading: Response to Intervention (RtI) and Multi-Tier Intervention in the Primary Grades](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/3) | framework | Five recommendations for primary-grade reading intervention using a multi-tier approach: screen all students for reading problems at the beginning and middle of the year (Moderate), provide differentiated reading instruction based on current assessments (Minimal), deliver intensive systematic instruction on up to three foundational reading skills in small groups for below-benchmark students (Strong), monitor tier 2 student progress monthly (Minimal), and deliver daily intensive instruction to tier 3 students showing minimal progress (Minimal). |
| 23 | [Reducing Behavior Problems in the Elementary School Classroom](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/4) | framework | Five recommendations for elementary classroom behavior management: identify specific conditions triggering problem behavior (Moderate), modify the classroom environment to remove triggers (Strong), actively teach socially and behaviorally appropriate replacement skills (Strong), leverage relationships with colleagues, families, and behavioral experts (Moderate), and implement schoolwide prevention strategies requiring shared responsibility (Moderate). |
| 24 | [Encouraging Girls in Math and Science](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/5) | framework | Five recommendations for broadening girls' participation in STEM: teach that academic abilities are expandable and improvable (Moderate), provide prescriptive informational feedback about student performance (Moderate), introduce female role models in math and science fields (Minimal), create learning environments that build curiosity and avoid gender stereotypes (Moderate), and integrate spatial skills training into instruction (Minimal). |
| 25 | [Effective Literacy and English Language Instruction for English Learners in the Elementary Grades](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/6) | framework | Five recommendations for literacy instruction with elementary English learners: screen for reading problems and monitor progress in English (Strong), provide intensive small-group reading interventions for at-risk students (Strong), deliver extensive and varied vocabulary instruction throughout the school day (Strong), make formal academic English development a key instructional goal beginning in primary grades (Minimal), and schedule regular peer-assisted learning for structured academic collaboration approximately 90 minutes weekly (Strong). |
| 26 | [Turning Around Chronically Low-Performing Schools](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/7) | framework | Four recommendations for school turnaround leaders: signal the need for dramatic change with leadership communicating urgency to staff and stakeholders; maintain a consistent focus on improving instruction by leveraging data to set goals and reassess learning continuously; make visible improvements early in the turnaround process to motivate staff and reduce resistance; and build a committed staff aligned with improvement objectives. |
| 27 | [Improving Adolescent Literacy: Effective Classroom and Intervention Practices](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/8) | framework | Five recommendations for grades 5–12 literacy: provide explicit vocabulary instruction (Strong), deliver direct and explicit comprehension strategy instruction (Strong), facilitate extended discussion of text meaning and interpretation (Moderate), increase student motivation and engagement with reading (Moderate), and provide intensive individualized reading interventions by trained specialists for struggling readers (Strong). |
| 28 | [Structuring Out-of-School Time to Improve Academic Achievement](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/10) | framework | Five recommendations for administrators and educators designing out-of-school programs: align the OST program academically with the school day (Minimal), maximize student participation and attendance (Minimal), adapt instruction to individual and small group needs (Moderate), provide engaging learning experiences (Minimal), and assess program performance using results to continuously improve quality (Minimal). |
| 29 | [Helping Students Navigate the Path to College: What High Schools Can Do](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/11) | framework | Five recommendations for high schools: provide college-preparatory coursework and ensure ninth graders understand college-readiness requirements; use ongoing assessments to monitor readiness and address skill gaps; foster college aspirations through supportive adult and peer networks; facilitate completion of essential college application steps (Moderate); and enhance financial literacy and support the financial aid application process (Moderate). |
| 30 | [Improving Reading Comprehension in Kindergarten Through 3rd Grade](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/14) | framework | Five recommendations for K–3 reading comprehension: train students to apply reading comprehension strategies (Strong), instruct students to leverage text structure for comprehension and retention (Moderate), facilitate substantive dialogue about textual meaning (Minimal), choose materials strategically to foster comprehension growth (Minimal), and establish an engaging and motivating context for reading instruction (Moderate). |
| 31 | [Developing Effective Fractions Instruction for Kindergarten Through 8th Grade](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/15) | framework | Five recommendations for fractions instruction grades K–8: build on students' informal understanding of sharing and proportionality; establish that fractions are numbers that expand the number system beyond whole numbers; support understanding of the reasoning behind fraction computation procedures; develop conceptual understanding of ratio, rate, and proportion before introducing cross-multiplication; and improve teachers' own fraction knowledge through professional development. Emphasizes number lines as a central teaching tool across grade levels. |
| 32 | [Teaching Elementary School Students to Be Effective Writers](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/17) | framework | Four recommendations for grades 1–6 writing instruction: provide daily time for students to write (Minimal), teach students to use the writing process for a variety of purposes (Strong), teach fluency with handwriting, spelling, sentence construction, typing, and word processing (Moderate), and create an engaged community of writers (Minimal). |
| 33 | [Foundational Skills to Support Reading for Understanding in Kindergarten Through 3rd Grade](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/21) | framework | Four recommendations for K–3 foundational reading: teach academic language skills including inferential language and vocabulary (Minimal), develop phonological awareness and sound-letter links (Strong), teach decoding, word analysis, and written word recognition (Strong), and ensure each student reads connected text daily to build accuracy, fluency, and comprehension (Moderate). |
| 34 | [Teaching Secondary Students to Write Effectively](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/22) | framework | Three recommendations for grades 6–12 writing instruction across all subject areas: explicitly teach appropriate writing strategies using a Model-Practice-Reflect instructional cycle (Strong), integrate writing and reading to emphasize key writing features (Moderate), and use assessments of student writing to inform instruction and feedback (Minimal). |
| 36 | [Preventing Dropout in Secondary Schools](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/24) | framework | Updated version of the 2008 dropout prevention guide, incorporating nine additional years of research. Four recommendations for grades 6–12: monitor all students and proactively intervene on early signs of attendance, behavior, or academic problems (Minimal); deliver tailored support to students who have fallen behind and face substantial barriers (Moderate); involve students through educational offerings bridging learning to college and career while building resilience (Strong); and for schools with many at-risk students, establish small personalized learning communities for better tracking and assistance (Moderate). |
| 39 | [Providing Reading Interventions for Students in Grades 4–9](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/PracticeGuide/29) | framework | Four recommendations for grades 4–9 reading intervention: build students' decoding skills for complex multisyllabic words (Strong), provide purposeful fluency-building activities (Strong), implement interconnected comprehension practices covering vocabulary, question-and-answer activities, text-gist routines, and self-monitoring (Strong), and provide opportunities for students to practice with challenging stretch text (Moderate). |
| 40 | [Knowledge is Power Program (KIPP) Charter Schools](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/688) | report | WWC Intervention Report on KIPP, a nationwide network of free open-enrollment college-preparatory schools in under-resourced communities providing approximately 60% more instructional time than traditional public schools. Reviews 4 studies meeting standards out of 6 eligible. Found positive effects on mathematics and English language arts achievement, potentially positive effects on science and social studies achievement, and no discernible effects on student progression for grades 5–12. |
| 41 | [Cognitive Tutor® Algebra I](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/655) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Cognitive Tutor® Algebra I, published by Carnegie Learning — a math curriculum combining textbooks with adaptive intelligent tutoring software. Reviews 6 studies meeting standards out of 18 eligible. Found positive effects on algebra achievement (grades 8–PS, Tier 1) and no discernible effects on general mathematics achievement in later grades (9–10). |
| 42 | [Intelligent Tutoring for Structure Strategy (ITSS)](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/703) | report | WWC Intervention Report on ITSS, a web-based supplemental program teaching students to identify and use text structure patterns (comparison, cause-effect, sequence, description, problem-solution) to improve literacy comprehension and recall in grades K–8. Reviews 2 studies meeting standards. Found positive effects on comprehension (grades 4–7, Tier 1). |
| 44 | [DreamBox Learning](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/627) | report | WWC Intervention Report on DreamBox Learning, a supplemental online adaptive mathematics program for grades K–5 that individualizes instruction by creating unique paths through the curriculum matching each student's level of comprehension and learning style. Reviews 1 study meeting standards. Found potentially positive effects on general mathematics achievement (K–1, Tier 3), based on a predominantly Hispanic, English learner, urban sample. |
| 45 | [READ 180®](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/665) | report | WWC Intervention Report on READ 180®, a reading program combining online and direct instruction, student assessment, and teacher professional development for struggling readers 2+ years below grade level in grades 4–10. Reviews 9 studies meeting standards out of 39 eligible. Found positive effects on comprehension and general literacy achievement, potentially positive effects on reading fluency, and no discernible effects on alphabetics. |
| 46 | [Achieve3000®](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/691) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Achieve3000®, a supplemental online literacy program providing differentiated nonfiction reading content for grades preK–12, targeting phonemic awareness, phonics, fluency, comprehension, vocabulary, and writing. Reviews 3 studies meeting standards out of 5 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on comprehension and general literacy achievement for adolescent readers (Tier 2). |
| 47 | [Success for All®](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/672) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Success for All® (SFA®), a whole-school reform model integrating curriculum, school culture, family, and community supports, emphasizing phonics for beginning readers and comprehension for all students in grades K–4. Reviews 9 studies meeting standards out of 49 eligible. Found positive effects on alphabetics, potentially positive effects on reading fluency, and mixed effects on comprehension and general reading achievement. |
| 48 | [Self-Regulated Strategy Development (SRSD)](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/680) | report | WWC Intervention Report on SRSD, an intervention for grades 2–12 that improves academic skills through a six-step process teaching specific academic strategies and self-regulation, progressing from teacher-directed instruction to independent student application. Reviews 10 studies meeting standards out of 16 eligible. Based on single-case design studies, found potentially positive effects on writing achievement for students with specific learning disabilities (Tier 3). |
| 51 | [Dual Enrollment Programs](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/671) | report | WWC Intervention Report on dual enrollment programs allowing high school students to take college courses and earn college credits, aimed at enhancing college access and degree completion for underrepresented students in grades 9–12. Reviews 5 studies meeting standards out of 35 eligible. Found positive effects (Tier 1) on degree attainment, college access/enrollment, credit accumulation, high school completion, and high school academic achievement. |
| 53 | [Leveled Literacy Intervention](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/679) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Leveled Literacy Intervention, a short-term supplementary small-group literacy program for grades K–2 struggling readers delivering explicit instruction in phonological awareness, phonics, fluency, vocabulary, comprehension, oral language, and writing. Reviews 2 studies meeting standards out of 10 eligible. Found positive effects on general reading achievement (Tier 1), potentially positive effects on reading fluency, and no discernible effects on alphabetics. |
| 54 | [Peer-Assisted Learning Strategies (PALS) — Beginning Reading](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/364) | report | WWC Intervention Report on PALS, a peer-tutoring program for grades K–6 in which students take turns acting as tutor and tutee, coaching and correcting one another through literacy problems. Reviews 3 studies meeting standards out of 13 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on alphabetics (K–1, Tier 2), no discernible effects on reading fluency, and mixed effects on comprehension. |
| 55 | [Good Behavior Game](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/728) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Good Behavior Game, a classroom management strategy for grades K–11 that organizes students into teams and rewards appropriate conduct to improve social skills and minimize disruptive behaviors. Reviews 16 studies meeting standards out of 31 eligible. Found positive effects on student behavior and teacher practice, potentially positive effects on writing conventions and writing productivity, and uncertain effects on academic achievement and school climate. |
| 56 | [Everyday Mathematics®](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/166) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Everyday Mathematics®, a core PreK–6 curriculum emphasizing real-life problem solving, student communication of mathematical thinking, technology use, collaborative learning, and parent involvement. Reviews 1 study meeting standards out of 34 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on general mathematics achievement for grades 3–5. |
| 57 | [Career Academies](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/70) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Career Academies, school-within-school high school programs where students take integrated career and academic courses while gaining real work experience through employer partnerships. Reviews 1 study meeting standards out of 9 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on school completion and staying in school, and no discernible effects on academic progress. |
| 58 | [Check & Connect](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/78) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Check & Connect, a dropout prevention strategy for grades 9–12 combining close monitoring of school performance with mentoring, case management, and individualized student supports. Reviews 2 studies meeting standards out of 3 eligible. Found positive effects on staying in school, potentially positive effects on progressing in school, and no discernible effects on completing school for students with learning, behavioral, or emotional disabilities. |
| 59 | [Teach for America (TFA)](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/663) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Teach for America, a highly selective alternative route to teacher certification placing non-traditionally trained teachers in high-need public schools with 5–7 weeks of summer training plus ongoing professional development and coaching. Reviews 7 studies meeting standards out of 24 eligible. Found positive effects on mathematics achievement, potentially positive effects on science achievement, and no discernible effects on English language arts or social studies for grades preK–12. |
| 60 | [MyTeachingPartner-Secondary (MTP-S)](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/635) | report | WWC Intervention Report on MTP-S, a web-based professional development program for grades 6–12 providing teachers access to a video library of high-quality teaching examples and individualized coaching approximately twice monthly, using the CLASS-S framework to define and observe effective teaching practice. Reviews 1 study meeting standards out of 3 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on general academic achievement for middle and high school students (Tier 3). |
| 61 | [Science Teachers Learning from Lesson Analysis (STeLLA)](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/716) | report | WWC Intervention Report on STeLLA, a professional development program by BSCS Science Learning for grades 4–5 teachers that improves student science achievement by enhancing teachers' science content knowledge and ability to explain concepts, identify learning objectives, and engage students in scientific thinking. Reviews 1 study meeting standards out of 3 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on science achievement compared with another yearlong teacher PD program (Tier 3). |
| 62 | [Dual Language Programs](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/726) | report | WWC Intervention Report on dual language programs providing content and literacy instruction through two languages (English and a partner language) in grades K–8, targeting academic achievement, bilingualism, biliteracy, and sociocultural competence. Reviews 2 studies meeting standards. Found potentially positive effects on English literacy achievement (Tier 2) and uncertain effects on mathematics and science achievement. |
| 63 | [Fraction Face-Off!](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/702) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Fraction Face-Off!, a supplemental math program for grade 4 students needing additional support with fraction problems, delivered individually or in small groups by teachers. Reviews 1 study meeting standards. Found potentially positive effects on general mathematics achievement, geometry and measurement, and number and operations (Tier 3), with a diverse sample (52% Black, 88% free/reduced-price lunch, 15% English learners). |
| 68 | [Repeated Reading](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/576) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Repeated Reading, an academic practice for grades 5–12 in which students read passages aloud multiple times with teacher support in a one-on-one setting to increase oral reading fluency. Reviews 3 studies meeting standards out of 15 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on reading comprehension for students with learning disabilities (Tier 3) and no discernible effects on alphabetics, reading fluency, or general reading achievement. |
| 70 | [Reading Recovery®](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/730) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Reading Recovery®, a one-on-one tutoring intervention for grade 1 students with low literacy achievement delivering 30-minute daily sessions over 12–20 weeks, tailoring instruction in phonemic awareness, phonics, vocabulary, fluency, comprehension, writing, and oral language. Reviews 2 studies meeting standards out of 20 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on literacy achievement, writing productivity, and receptive communication skills, with uncertain long-term effects on mathematics and academic achievement. |
| 71 | [Reading Apprenticeship®](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/727) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Reading Apprenticeship®, a professional development program for grades 7–12 teachers that improves student literacy and also targets social-emotional outcomes including belonging, growth mindset, and self-efficacy. Reviews 5 studies meeting standards out of 9 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on science achievement and grade point average for grade 9 students (Tier 2) and uncertain effects on other literacy, mathematics, social studies, and behavioral outcomes. |
| 72 | [eMINTS Comprehensive Program](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/705) | report | WWC Intervention Report on the eMINTS Comprehensive Program, a professional development program for grades 4–8 teachers offering structured PD, coaching, and support for integrating technology into the classroom. Reviews 2 studies meeting standards out of 8 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on general mathematics achievement (Tier 2) and no discernible effects on literacy achievement. |
| 75 | [Bottom Line](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/714) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Bottom Line, an intensive advising program for high school students from low-income households covering college applications, financial aid, and institution selection, plus ongoing campus support for up to six years. Reviews 1 study meeting standards. Found potentially positive effects on college enrollment and college progression for low-income and first-generation college-going students (Tier 3). |
| 77 | [I CAN Learn® Primary Mathematics](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/676) | report | WWC Intervention Report on I CAN Learn® Primary Mathematics, a computer-based math curriculum for middle school through college delivering mastery-based interactive lessons that students complete individually at their own pace. Reviews 1 study meeting standards out of 7 eligible. Found no discernible effects on general mathematics achievement for eighth-grade students. |
| 78 | [Fast Track: Elementary School](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/628) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Fast Track: Elementary School, a comprehensive multi-component intervention including the PATHS curriculum, parent groups, social skills training, home visiting, peer-pairing, and academic tutoring to reduce conduct problems and improve academic, behavioral, and social outcomes. Reviews 1 study meeting standards. Found potentially positive effects on emotional/internal behavior, external behavior, reading achievement, and social outcomes for children with or at risk for emotional disturbance (Tier 3). |
| 80 | [Fast ForWord®](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/173) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Fast ForWord®, a computer-based reading program running 30–100 minutes daily for 4–16 weeks that develops the cognitive skills necessary for successful reading and learning in grades K–10. Reviews 8 studies meeting standards out of 29 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on reading fluency and comprehension, and no discernible effects on alphabetics or general literacy achievement for adolescent learners. |
| 82 | [Lexia Reading](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/274) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Lexia Reading, a computerized reading program providing phonics instruction and independent practice in basic reading skills for grades K–1 across five areas identified by the National Reading Panel. Reviews 3 studies meeting standards out of 4 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on alphabetics and comprehension, and no discernible effects on reading fluency or general reading achievement. |
| 86 | [Class-Wide Function-Related Intervention Teams (CW-FIT)](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/729) | report | WWC Intervention Report on CW-FIT, a classroom management strategy for PreK–10 designed to create a positive learning environment by decreasing disruptive behavior and improving students' social skills through team-based incentive structures. Reviews 8 studies meeting standards out of 19 eligible. Found positive effects on student behavior (Tier 1) and positive effects on teacher practice (Tier 3). |
| 87 | [Job Corps](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/247) | report | WWC Intervention Report on Job Corps, a federally funded residential education and job training program for economically disadvantaged youth in grades 11–12 offering remedial education, GED preparation, vocational training, job placement, and other supports for up to 2 years. Reviews 1 study meeting standards. Found potentially positive effects on school completion and no discernible effects on academic progression. |
| 89 | [ACT/SAT Test Preparation and Coaching Programs](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/664) | report | WWC Intervention Report on ACT/SAT test preparation programs for grades 10–12 providing test familiarization, general and specific test-taking strategies, and practice drills aimed at increasing scores on college entrance exams. Reviews 6 studies meeting standards out of 26 eligible. Found positive effects on general academic achievement for high school students (Tier 1). |
| 90 | [University of Chicago School Mathematics Project (UCSMP) Algebra](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/641) | report | WWC Intervention Report on UCSMP Algebra, a one-year course for grades 7–10 emphasizing algebra applications, using statistics and geometry to develop linear equations and inequalities with intentional connections across mathematical domains. Reviews 2 studies meeting standards out of 3 eligible. Found potentially positive effects on algebra and general mathematics achievement (Tier 3). |
| 93 | [First Step to Success](https://ies.ed.gov/ncee/wwc/InterventionReport/179) | report | WWC Intervention Report on First Step to Success, an early intervention program for grades K–3 at-risk children employing a trained behavior coach across three modules — screening, classroom intervention, and parent training — to prevent aggressive or antisocial behavioral patterns. Reviews 2 studies meeting standards out of 6 eligible. Found positive effects on external behavior, potentially positive effects on emotional/internal behavior, social outcomes, and academic performance, and no discernible effects on reading achievement. |

| 140 | [Community Schools Impact on Student Outcomes: Evidence From California](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/ca-community-schools-impact-student-outcomes-report) | report | Learning Policy Institute study by Swain, Leung-Gagné, Maier, and Rubinstein (2025) evaluating California's Community Schools Partnership Program (CCSPP), a $4.1 billion state initiative. Analyzes 458 schools using matched difference-in-differences methodology across 2018–19 through 2023–24. Finds 30% greater reduction in chronic absenteeism, ~15% decline in suspensions, and academic gains of 0.06 SD in math and 0.05 SD in ELA (~43 and ~36 additional learning days). Benefits were disproportionately large for Black students, English learners, and socioeconomically disadvantaged students. |
| 141 | [The State of the Teacher Workforce](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/state-of-teacher-workforce-interactive-0) | report | Learning Policy Institute interactive report (updated July 2024) providing state-by-state analysis of teacher workforce conditions across all 50 U.S. states. | 148 | [Funding Gaps 2018](https://edtrust.org/resource/funding-gaps-2018/) | report | EdTrust report by Morgan and Amerikaner (2018) documenting school funding disparities across U.S. states and districts. Finds districts serving predominantly students of color receive ~$1,800 (13%) less per student in state and local funding than majority-white districts; high-poverty districts receive ~$1,000 (7%) less than low-poverty districts. Gap widens to $2,000 per student (16%) when adjusted for additional costs of supporting low-income students. Documents state-by-state variation and highlights states achieving more equitable funding. |
| 149 | [Teacher Turnover in the United States: Who Moves, Who Leaves, and Why](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/teacher-turnover-united-states-report) | report | This comprehensive analysis examines why approximately 1 in 7 public school teachers annually leave their schools or exit the profession, using national data from 2020–22. The report finds that turnover rates have increased roughly 27% since the early 1990s, with disparities concentrated in schools serving predominantly low-income students and students of color. Three primary factors significantly influence retention: teacher salaries, effective school leadership, and overall job satisfaction tied to classroom autonomy and collegial support. |
| 150 | [Supporting and Sustaining a Diverse Teacher Workforce](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/diverse-teacher-workforce-report) | report | This report examines why teachers of color leave the profession at higher rates than their white counterparts and identifies systemic barriers to their retention. Using national survey data from 2017–2021, researchers found that teachers of color face less access to comprehensive preparation, competitive salaries, mentoring support, and positive working environments. The study emphasizes that while recruiting more educators of color matters, retention through improved conditions and support systems is equally critical to building a stable, diverse teaching force. |
| 152 | [Community Schools as an Effective School Improvement Strategy: A Review of the Evidence](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/community-schools-effective-school-improvement-report) | report | This comprehensive report synthesizes findings from 143 rigorous research studies examining how community schools impact student and school outcomes, identifying four core pillars of effective implementation: integrated student supports, expanded learning time, family and community engagement, and collaborative leadership. The study demonstrates that community schools help disadvantaged students overcome barriers to learning by integrating academic instruction with health services, youth development, and community partnerships. The evidence base meets federal requirements under the Every Student Succeeds Act, providing policymakers with research-backed guidance for implementing community schools as an equitable school improvement strategy. |
| 154 | [Reducing Chronic Absenteeism: Lessons from Community Schools](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/chronic-absenteeism-community-schools-report) | report | This report examines how four California community schools successfully reduced chronic absenteeism following the pandemic, when national rates had nearly doubled to 28% by 2022. Key strategies included strengthening family engagement through outreach and home visits, building student connections through mentorship, systematically tracking attendance data, implementing tiered support systems, and leveraging community partnerships to address underlying barriers like housing and mental health needs. The findings emphasize that proactive and preventive approaches combined with trusting relationships prove more effective than punitive measures in addressing attendance challenges. |
| 155 | [Strengthening the Educator Pipeline Through Service Scholarships: California's Golden State Teacher Grant Program](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/ca-golden-state-teacher-grant-program-report) | report | This report evaluates California's Golden State Teacher Grant Program, which has distributed over $570 million since 2020–21 to help aspiring teachers afford preparation costs in exchange for committing to teach in high-need schools for two years, supporting more than 28,000 candidates and likely contributing to a 23% increase in California-prepared teachers earning credentials. Nearly 75% of survey respondents cited the grant as influential in their decision to pursue teaching, with particularly strong impact among candidates of color and career changers. The report recommends stable long-term funding benchmarked to cover significant portions of preparation costs, improved implementation support, and better data collection systems to track long-term outcomes. |
| 158 | [Community Schools Impact on Student Outcomes: Evidence From California](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/ca-community-schools-impact-student-outcomes-report) | report | This report evaluates California's $4.1 billion Community Schools Partnership Program, the nation's largest state-level community schools initiative launched in 2021, using a matched difference-in-differences methodology comparing 458 CCSPP schools to similar non-recipient schools from 2018–19 to 2023–24. Researchers found that community school approaches led to a meaningful reduction in chronic absence averaging 30% greater than comparison schools, along with notable improvements in suspension rates and test scores. The most substantial gains benefited historically underserved students including Black students, English learners, and economically disadvantaged youth. |
| 159 | [Teacher Residencies in Texas: Supporting Successful Implementation](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/texas-teacher-residencies-implementation-report) | report | This report examines Texas's High-Quality, Sustainable Residencies Program, which funded over 85 teacher residency programs from 2021–2024 using COVID-19 relief money and provided stipends for more than 2,000 residents. The report concludes with six policy recommendations including requirements for research-based residency features, support for mentor teacher pipelines, and targeted funding for rural and high-poverty districts facing acute teacher shortages. |
| 160 | [Diversifying the Teaching Profession: How to Recruit and Retain Teachers of Color](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/diversifying-teaching-profession-report) | report | This report examines barriers that impede teachers of color from entering and remaining in the profession, while documenting how such teachers significantly benefit student outcomes. The study identifies three primary intervention areas: high-retention pathways into teaching through scholarships and residencies; hiring strategies that include diverse committees and early recruitment; and improving school conditions through better leadership preparation. |
| 161 | [Design Principles for Teacher Preparation: Enacting the Science of Learning and Development](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/sold-design-principles-teacher-preparation-report) | report | This report presents five design principles for teacher preparation programs that incorporate findings from neuroscience, developmental psychology, and learning sciences to help future educators support whole child development. The framework emphasizes that teacher candidates must themselves experience the very kinds of teaching strategies they are expected to develop for their pupils, ensuring teachers can authentically model approaches like inquiry-based learning and restorative practices. The principles address curriculum grounded in learning science, equitable educator mindsets, experiential learning opportunities, pedagogical alignment between university coursework and school placements, and the creation of supportive professional learning communities. |
| 163 | [Developing Assessment Systems That Support Teaching and Learning: Recommendations for Federal Support](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/developing-assessment-systems-federal-support-report) | report | This report examines how the federal government can support states in transforming their assessment systems beyond traditional end-of-year multiple-choice tests, noting that more than 20 states are involved in efforts to transform one or more aspects of their assessment systems but face substantial barriers to federal approval. The authors identify three key recommendations for federal executive action: updating the Department of Education's peer review process to align with ESSA's encouragement of innovative assessments, strengthening the Innovative Assessment Demonstration Authority program to reduce burdensome requirements, and expanding funding pathways to incentivize state-level innovation in assessment design. |
| 164 | [School Funding Effectiveness: Evidence From California's Local Control Funding Formula](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/school-funding-effectiveness-ca-lcff-report) | report | This comprehensive study examines how California's 2013 Local Control Funding Formula—which directed $18 billion in increased state support to high-need districts over eight years—affected student outcomes across the state's 1,000 districts and 10,000 schools. Tracking 6.2 million K-12 students from 1995–96 through 2018–19, the research finds that a $1,000 per-pupil spending increase yielded full grade-level improvements in math and reading after three years, boosted high school graduation rates by 8.2 percentage points, and decreased suspensions and expulsions particularly for Black students. Instructional spending—class size reduction, teacher salary increases, and reduced turnover—accounted for roughly 84% of the variation in funding effectiveness. |
| 165 | [Lost Opportunities: How Disparate School Discipline Continues to Drive Differences in the Opportunity to Learn](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/crdc-school-discipline-report) | report | This report examines how out-of-school suspensions and other exclusionary discipline practices create substantial learning loss, with particularly severe impacts on students of color, secondary students, and those with disabilities. During the 2015–16 school year alone, the nation lost approximately 11.4 million instructional days due to suspensions—the equivalent of 62,596 years of lost learning. The research documents widespread racial disparities in discipline outcomes and significant failures by districts to report school-policing data as required by federal law. |
| 166 | [Long-Term English Learners in California](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/ca-long-term-english-learners-report) | report | This report examines approximately 330,000 California students classified as English learners for 7 or more years, analyzing their demographics, school contexts, and academic outcomes compared to peers. The research reveals that long-term English learners are disproportionately boys (56%), economically disadvantaged (89%), and have higher special education needs (28%), while attending schools with fewer resources and lower graduation rates. Key recommendations include ensuring adequate schooling resources and whole-child supports, addressing teacher shortages in high-poverty schools, better identifying early learning needs for students with disabilities, and conducting further research on graduation barriers for this vulnerable population. |
| 167 | [Investing for Student Success: Lessons From State School Finance Reforms](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/investing-student-success-school-finance-reforms-report) | report | This report examines how U.S. public schools are inequitably funded through reliance on local property taxes and analyzes reform efforts in Connecticut, Massachusetts, New Jersey, and North Carolina that successfully improved educational resources and outcomes. The research demonstrates that when more money is spent on education, especially for students from low-income families, achievement and graduation rates improve alongside life outcomes like employment and wages. The report recommends federal and state actions—including equalizing funding allocations, ensuring qualified teachers reach high-poverty schools, and investing in quality preschool—as essential steps toward creating equitable learning opportunities for all students. |
| 168 | [Advancing Integration and Equity Through Magnet Schools](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/advancing-integration-equity-magnet-schools-report) | report | This report examines how magnet schools—public schools offering specialized programs to attract diverse student populations—can combat the alarming resegregation occurring in U.S. schools. The authors identify key components for success, including "first door" strategies like intentional family outreach and inclusive enrollment, plus "second door" approaches such as culturally responsive curriculum and restorative discipline practices. The report provides policy recommendations at federal, state, district, and school levels to help create and sustain magnet schools that achieve both integration and positive academic outcomes for all students. |
| 169 | [Instructionally Relevant Assessment Systems: What is the Role of Performance Assessments?](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/instructionally-relevant-assessment-systems-report) | report | This report examines how performance assessments—tasks requiring human scoring like constructed responses and essays—can transform state assessment systems to prioritize positive instructional impact alongside accountability functions. The author argues that because teachers inevitably take cues from state-level assessments to shape their instruction, assessment designers should intentionally leverage this influence by incorporating authentic performance tasks that signal high-quality teaching practices. The report provides system leaders with five strategic recommendations for balancing performance assessments with other instruments, emphasizing that well-designed tasks can generate reliable comparable scores while simultaneously improving what students learn and how teachers teach. |
| 170 | [The Promise of Performance Assessments: Innovations in High School Learning and Higher Education Admissions](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/promise-performance-assessments-report) | report | This report examines how schools and colleges are adopting performance-based assessments—such as portfolios, capstone projects, and senior defenses—to evaluate deeper learning skills needed for college and career success. The authors document that more than 900 colleges have made standardized tests optional and are increasingly interested in reviewing broader student work samples for admission decisions. A key finding is that these assessments show particular promise for better reflecting the achievements and potential of historically underserved students, potentially addressing longstanding disparities in standardized testing outcomes across racial and socioeconomic lines. |
| 172 | [Community Schools in Lynwood Unified: Building Capacity for Districtwide Implementation](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/ca-community-schools-lynwood-unified-report) | report | This report examines how Lynwood Unified School District successfully scaled community schools across all 17 campuses by strategically developing community school coordinators as implementation leaders, leveraging $24 million in state funding to expand from a single pilot school to a districtwide initiative. The district achieved measurable improvements including a one-third reduction in chronic absence, enhanced academic performance, and a 15.5% decline in suspensions within two years. The research highlights five critical implementation strategies including data-driven needs assessments, structured professional learning communities, and coaching networks that positioned coordinators as essential change agents rather than peripheral support roles. |
| 173 | [State Strategies for Investing in Community Schools](https://learningpolicyinstitute.org/product/state-strategies-investing-in-community-schools-report) | report | This report examines how states are funding and supporting community schools by identifying three primary approaches: ongoing funding through school financing formulas (Maryland, New York), competitive grant programs (California, Illinois, New Mexico, Vermont), and capacity-building supports like certification processes (Florida, Georgia). State investments range from $3.4 million to $4.1 billion, with most states prioritizing schools serving high-poverty populations. Key findings show states are increasingly investing in evidence-based strategies including integrated student supports, expanded learning time, family engagement, and collaborative leadership structures that align with trauma-informed practices. |
| 175 | [Opportunities Denied: High-Achieving Black and Latino Students Lack Access to Advanced Math](https://edtrust.org/rti/opportunities-denied-high-achieving-black-and-latino-students-lack-access-to-advanced-math/) | report | High-achieving Black and Latino students and students from low-income backgrounds who pass Algebra I in eighth grade still enroll in advanced high school math at lower rates than their peers. Developed jointly with Just Equations, this brief documents enrollment disparities and identifies differences in teacher and counselor practices as contributing factors to these access gaps. |
| 176 | [Social, Emotional, and Academic Development Through an Equity Lens](https://edtrust.org/rti/social-emotional-and-academic-development-through-an-equity-lens/) | report | Schools often narrowly focus on changing student behavior rather than building relationships and supportive learning environments, disproportionately affecting students of color and low-income students. This framework guides school and district leaders to shift attention from "fixing kids" to examining adult beliefs, school policies, and district practices that create equitable learning environments, ensuring all students experience belonging, challenging opportunities, and appropriate supports. |
| 178 | [If You Listen, We Will Stay: Why Teachers of Color Leave and How to Disrupt Teacher Turnover](https://edtrust.org/rti/if-you-listen-we-will-stay/) | report | Drawing on focus groups with Black and Latino teachers and case studies of schools successfully retaining faculty of color, the report identifies five key barriers teachers face and five actions effective schools employ to support diverse educators. |
| 180 | [Inequities in Advanced Coursework](https://edtrust.org/rti/inequities-in-advanced-coursework/) | report | This report examines barriers preventing Black and Latino students from accessing AP, IB, and dual enrollment programs, finding that while these students succeed when given opportunities, they remain underrepresented due to three compounding factors: schools serving predominantly students of color offering fewer advanced courses, lower overall enrollment in those courses, and racially diverse schools actively denying access. The report provides recommendations for state and district leaders to expand access and improve equity in advanced coursework enrollment. |
| 182 | [Promising Practices: Smart State Strategies for Building Intensive Tutoring Systems](https://edtrust.org/rti/promising-practices-smart-state-strategies-for-building-intensive-tutoring-systems/) | report | This brief examines how states are implementing high-impact tutoring programs to address learning disruptions caused by school closures. The publication identifies promising state strategies including workforce development, research-based program standards, and professional development, while emphasizing equitable access to tutoring for underserved students. |
| 183 | [How School Discipline Impacts Students' Social, Emotional, and Academic Development (SEAD)](https://edtrust.org/rti/how-school-discipline-impacts-students-social-emotional-and-academic-development-sead/) | report | This brief examines how school discipline policies affect student development, highlighting that harmful practices like corporal punishment, suspensions, and expulsions disproportionately impact students of color and those with disabilities. The report advocates for evidence-based approaches such as restorative justice and emphasizes the importance of implementing discipline policies through a racial equity lens to create safe, supportive learning environments. |
| 184 | [5 Things for Advocates to Know About Chronic Absenteeism](https://edtrust.org/rti/5-things-for-advocates-to-know-about-chronic-absenteeism/) | report | This brief documents the rising epidemic of chronic school absence, noting that 16 million students nationally are now chronically absent — missing 10% or more of school days — with disproportionate impact on low-income students, students of color, multilingual learners, and students with disabilities. The report advocates for evidence-based approaches addressing root causes rather than punitive measures, and highlights the importance of prevention, early intervention, and authentic partnerships with families and communities. |
| 185 | [How Mental Health Supports Impact Social, Emotional, and Academic Development (SEAD)](https://edtrust.org/rti/how-mental-health-supports-impact-social-emotional-and-academic-development-sead/) | report | This brief examines how school-based mental health services support student success, highlighting significant disparities in access for students of color, low-income students, and students with disabilities. The authors present CDC data showing alarming mental health challenges among high school students and emphasize that district leaders must implement mental health support services rooted in a race-based lens to address these inequities. |
| 186 | [Future of Assessments: Centering Equity and the Lived Experiences of Students, Families, and Educators](https://edtrust.org/rti/future-of-assessments-centering-equity-and-the-lived-experiences-of-students-families-and-educators/) | report | This report examines statewide assessment practices through focus groups with students, families, educators, and administrators, revealing that stakeholders want assessment data from multiple sources, believe assessments are just one measure of college readiness, and seek timely and actionable results alongside improved resource allocation to underserved schools. The report proposes four equity pillars to guide future federal education policy and assessment design. |
| 187 | [Holding Students Back: An Inequitable and Ineffective Response to Unfinished Learning](https://edtrust.org/rti/holding-students-back-an-inequitable-and-ineffective-response-to-unfinished-learning/) | report | This factsheet examines grade retention as a response to pandemic-related learning loss, arguing that while retention may show short-term test score improvements, research demonstrates it produces negative long-term academic, social, and emotional outcomes — particularly for Black, Latino, and Native students. The analysis recommends evidence-based alternatives including targeted tutoring, expanded learning time, and strong teacher-student relationships. |
| 189 | [The Future of Statewide Summative Assessments](https://edtrust.org/rti/the-future-of-statewide-summative-assessments/) | report | EdTrust identifies statewide summative assessments as an essential tool to achieve education equity by providing comparable data across students and schools. The organization presents four equity pillars for improving assessments — ensuring consistent expectations, encouraging inclusive assessments, providing timely results, and making results meaningful — offering advocates and policymakers recommendations for enhancing assessment systems to better serve students, families, and educators. |
| 193 | [Building a Diverse & Strong Educator Workforce for Students](https://edtrust.org/rti/building-a-diverse-strong-educator-workforce-for-students/) | report | This resource highlights a significant disparity in educator demographics: students of color make up more than 50% of the K-12 public school student population while teachers of color represent less than 20% of educators. Students from underserved backgrounds are four times more likely to attend schools with larger percentages of uncertified and inexperienced teachers, contributing to disparities in discipline rates, graduation outcomes, and educational access. |
| 194 | [Access Granted: School Funding Between Schools in Districts](https://edtrust.org/rti/access-granted-school-funding-between-schools-in-districts/) | report | EdTrust analyzed newly available school-by-school spending data required by the Every Student Succeeds Act to examine funding equity within districts. The analysis reveals that in 97% of districts included, state and local spending was flat or regressive for all student groups examined, with very few students of color, low-income students, or English learners attending schools where funding is allocated to meet their additional needs. |
| 195 | [Reassessing ESSA Implementation: An Equity Analysis of School Accountability Systems](https://edtrust.org/rti/reassessing-essa-implementation-an-equity-analysis/) | report | EdTrust examined how states implement federal school accountability requirements under the Every Student Succeeds Act, finding that states have a mixed record of centering equity in school ratings. The analysis identifies significant gaps in how states identify low-performing schools and support improvement efforts, and reveals that while some states show promising practices, most have failed to connect school identification processes to robust support systems that would meaningfully improve outcomes for historically underserved students. |
| 201 | [Evidence for ESSA: Reading Partners](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/reading-partners/) | report | Reading Partners is a one-to-one tutoring program delivered by trained community volunteers who work with struggling readers in grades 2–5 for two 45-minute sessions per week. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on one MDRC randomized controlled trial (n=1,166) showing students gained significantly more on SAT-10 comprehension and other outcomes (effect size +0.10) compared to peers, two-thirds of whom received alternative literacy services. |
| 202 | [Evidence for ESSA: Success for All](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/success-for-all-whole-class/) | report | Success for All is a comprehensive K–5 school reform program combining phonics instruction, cooperative learning, small-group tutoring, and frequent regrouping for high-poverty schools. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on six U.S. studies (n=8,849 students across 207 schools) including two RCTs, with an average effect size of +0.29 and particularly robust effects on foundational reading skills and for lower-achieving students. |
| 203 | [Evidence for ESSA: Reading Recovery](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/reading-recovery/) | report | Reading Recovery targets first-graders in the lowest 20–30% of their cohort, delivering daily 30-minute one-to-one lessons from specially trained teachers covering fluency, decoding, phonemic awareness, and guided reading. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on four qualifying studies (n=7,756 students) with an average effect size of +0.43 on standardized reading assessments including ITBS, Woodcock, and Gates measures. |
| 204 | [Evidence for ESSA: Peer-Assisted Learning Strategies (PALS) — Reading](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/peer-assisted-learning-strategies-pals-reading-elementary-whole-class/) | report | PALS is a peer-tutoring approach where first-grade student pairs alternate teacher and learner roles, working through a structured sequence of phonemic awareness, phonics, sound blending, and passage reading with error correction. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on three studies (n=1,501 students) with an average effect size of +0.23 on Woodcock reading scales. |
| 205 | [Evidence for ESSA: Enhanced Core Reading Instruction (ECRI)](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/enhanced-core-reading-instruction-ecri/) | report | ECRI enhances Tier 2 supplemental reading instruction for struggling first graders by providing teacher professional development and small-group sessions (3–5 students) targeting phonemic awareness, decoding, fluency, and irregular word reading. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on one study (n=811 students in Oregon and Massachusetts) with an average effect size of +0.24 on Woodcock-Johnson and SAT-10 measures. |
| 206 | [Evidence for ESSA: Reading Corps](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/reading-corps/) | report | Reading Corps is an AmeriCorps program that places trained literacy tutors in K–3 classrooms to deliver 20-minute daily supplemental instruction using research-based protocols. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on two RCTs (n=798 students in Minnesota and Wisconsin) with an average effect size of +0.36, with particularly strong gains in Letter Sound Fluency (+0.74) and Nonsense Word Fluency (+0.62) for K–1 students. |
| 207 | [Evidence for ESSA: Targeted Reading Instruction (TRI)](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/targeted-reading-instruction-tri/) | report | TRI is a one-to-one tutoring intervention that uses webcam technology to train educators to deliver 15-minute daily individualized reading sessions for struggling K–1 readers, covering oral language, decoding, writing, comprehension, vocabulary, and fluency. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on two studies (n=471 students) with an average effect size of +0.51 on letter-word identification, word attack, and passage comprehension measures. |
| 208 | [Evidence for ESSA: Sound Partners — Struggling Readers](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/sound-partners-struggling-readers/) | report | Sound Partners is a one-on-one paraprofessional tutoring intervention for kindergarten and first-grade struggling readers, providing 30 minutes of daily instruction four days per week for 18–20 weeks in phonics, phonemic awareness, sight words, and decodable text reading. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on two qualifying studies (n=488 students) with an average effect size of +0.58, with sustained gains confirmed at two-year follow-up assessments. |
| 209 | [Evidence for ESSA: QuickReads — Struggling Readers](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/quickreads-struggling-readers/) | report | QuickReads is a supplemental tutoring program for struggling readers in grades 2–5 that pairs students for 30-minute daily sessions four days per week over approximately 20 weeks, emphasizing repeated reading, phonics, and comprehension. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on three studies (n=483 students) with an average effect size of +0.21 on GORT and Woodcock reading assessments. |
| 210 | [Evidence for ESSA: Air Reading](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/air-reading/) | report | Air Reading is a virtual tutoring program for grades 1–6 that delivers explicit, skills-based reading instruction in small groups of up to four students meeting four times weekly for 30 minutes, with consistent tutors using diagnostic assessment to guide individualized instruction. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on two RCTs (n=740 students in Texas and Louisiana) with a weighted average effect size of +0.20 across NWEA MAP, DIBELS, LEAP, and STAAR assessments. |
| 211 | [Evidence for ESSA: Building Assets Reducing Risks (BARR) — Reading](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/building-assets-reducing-risks-barr-reading/) | report | BARR is a training and coaching model for K–12 schools built around structured teacher team meetings, data-driven student support, community engagement, and professional development to improve student outcomes. Evidence for ESSA rates the reading component Strong based on three randomized studies (n=9,398 students) with an average effect size of +0.08, with stronger impacts of +0.21 for struggling readers in high school settings. |
| 212 | [Evidence for ESSA: Intelligent Tutoring for the Structure Strategy (ITSS)](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/intelligent-tutoring-for-the-structure-strategy-itss-secondary/) | report | ITSS is a web-based intelligent tutoring system that teaches nonfiction text comprehension through text structure categorization, delivered in 30–45-minute weekly sessions in language arts classes with paraprofessional support. | 213 | [Evidence for ESSA: Zearn Math](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/zearn-math/) | report | Zearn Math is a nonprofit adaptive online supplement for K–8 math instruction featuring guided video lessons using a Concrete-to-Pictorial-to-Abstract progression with mastery-based quizzes. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on three studies (including one RCT with 10,000+ students in Texas) with an average effect size of +0.09 on NWEA MAP assessments, with strongest results when schools received dedicated implementation support including professional development and coaching. |
| 214 | [Evidence for ESSA: ASSISTments](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/assistments/) | report | ASSISTments is an online homework tool for grades 7–8 that provides students with math assistance during practice while giving teachers real-time assessment data on student progress. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on two RCTs (a Maine study and a North Carolina replication with ~6,000 students) with a combined mean effect size of +0.13 on mathematics assessments. |
| 215 | [Evidence for ESSA: DreamBox Math](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/dreambox-learning/) | report | DreamBox Math is an adaptive game-based online platform that delivers personalized, standards-aligned K–8 math instruction as a supplement to classroom teaching. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on two RCTs (n=13,320 students in California and North Carolina) with an average effect size of +0.10 on the Number Knowledge Test and state assessments, with strongest impacts for K–2 students. |
| 216 | [Evidence for ESSA: ST Math (Spatial Temporal Math)](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/st-math-spatial-temporal-math/) | report | ST Math is a supplemental visual problem-solving program for grades 3–6 built on the science of how the brain learns, using animation and game-based tasks to develop mathematical understanding without language barriers. | 219 | [Evidence for ESSA: Saga Math](https://evidenceforessa.org/program/saga-math/) | report | Saga Math is a high-dosage tutoring intervention for grades 9–10 offering traditional (1:2–1:4) or technology-enhanced formats (1:8 alternating direct tutoring with ALEKS computer-assisted learning), delivered three times weekly for 90 minutes total to supplement regular math instruction. Evidence for ESSA rates it Strong based on three studies (n=5,396+ predominantly Black and Latinx students in Chicago) with an average effect size of +0.14, plus increased math GPAs, reduced course failures, and persistent gains one to two years post-intervention. |
