| 241 | [Tracing Systematic Errors to Personalize Recommendations in Single Digit Multiplication and Beyond](https://jedm.educationaldatamining.org/index.php/JEDM/article/view/382) | paper | Presents the Systematic Error Tracing (SET) model, which uses a bigraph linking errors to underlying causes—inspired by Ising model physics—to identify persistent mistake patterns in elementary multiplication. Applied to 25,000+ incorrect responses from 335 students on the Math Garden platform, SET outperforms baseline approaches for generating personalized learning recommendations. |
| 262 | [Use of Learning Analytics in K–12 Mathematics Education: Systematic Scoping Review of the Impact on Teaching and Learning](https://learning-analytics.info/index.php/JLA/article/view/8299) | paper | Systematic scoping review of 19 studies on learning analytics in K–12 mathematics, finding the field is emerging with applications across diverse curricula. Teaching approaches using analytics-informed data-based decision-making concentrate primarily on supervision and guidance, with generally favorable outcomes for student achievement and notably stronger benefits for higher-performing students. The authors introduce the concept of "guiding analytics for learners" as a framework for leveraging analytics to foster self-directed learning. |
| 272 | [Deliberative Interactions for Socially Shared Regulation in Collaborative Learning: An AI-Driven Learning Analytics Study](https://learning-analytics.info/index.php/JLA/article/view/8393) | paper | Analyzes 2,125 utterances from video-recorded collaborative tasks involving 30 Finnish secondary students to examine socially shared regulation patterns. Two distinct deliberation patterns emerged: a Plan and Implementation Approach associated with adaptive group regulation, and a Trials and Failure Approach linked to maladaptive patterns, with many groups failing to recognize or respond to emerging regulatory needs. The study combines epistemic network analysis with multimodal data and AI-assisted coding, advancing understanding of how groups manage cognitive and emotional regulation during collaborative problem-solving. |
| 273 | [Uncovering Engagement Profiles of Young Learners in K–8 Education through Learning Analytics](https://learning-analytics.info/index.php/JLA/article/view/8133) | paper | Applies k-means cluster analysis to e-learning trace data from grades 1–8 to identify seven distinct student engagement profiles, finding that some profiles were absent at specific grades, indicating shifting engagement behaviors across development. Younger students showed stronger interest in game-based learning components while middle school students prioritized exam preparation, and most students focused on particular platform features rather than using all available resources. The study generates seven design considerations for effective K–8 e-learning platforms. |
| 277 | [Developing a Stealth Assessment System Using a Continuous Conjunctive Model](https://learning-analytics.info/index.php/JLA/article/view/7639) | paper | Creates a mathematics educational game called The Nomads targeting rational number arithmetic and applies a continuous conjunctive model (CCM) for cognitive diagnostic assessment of player data. Unlike traditional diagnostic models, CCM generates parameters at the attribute level and offers more parsimonious diagnoses using continuous variables, lending itself well to the dynamic nature of game-based learning. Learners' attribute mastery improved during gameplay and learners benefited from scaffolding on three of the game's instructed attributes. |
| 279 | [The Science Student Electronic Exit Ticket (SEET) System: Visualizations to Help Teachers Notice and Reflect on Classroom Inequalities](https://learning-analytics.info/index.php/JLA/article/view/8199) | paper | Evaluates the SEET system's equity analytics visualizations with 42 middle school science teachers across two groups in a two-month professional development program, using diary entries and interviews. Only equity visualizations — showing participation data disaggregated by gender and race/ethnicity — prompted teacher reflections on diverse student experiences, though teachers consistently rated whole-class visualizations as more practical to use. The study highlights a tension between equity-focused and usability-focused design priorities in teacher-facing learning analytics tools. |
| 281 | [The Scope of Multimodal Learning Analytics in K–8: A Systematic Review](https://learning-analytics.info/index.php/JLA/article/view/8505) | paper | Scoping review of 14 peer-reviewed empirical studies (2011–2023) on multimodal learning analytics in elementary K–8 education, finding varied applications but consistent gaps: studies rarely describe data fusion techniques or address ethics and transparency requirements. The review concludes that multimodal learning analytics holds promise for improving student outcomes but that the field requires more guidance and shared understanding for conducting rigorous work in K–8 contexts. Findings point to a need for clearer reporting standards and ethical frameworks tailored to young learners. |
| 282 | [The Relationship between Wellbeing and Academic Achievement: A Comprehensive Cross-Sectional Analysis of System-Wide Data From 2016–2019](https://learning-analytics.info/index.php/JLA/article/view/8357) | paper | Analyzes Wellbeing Engagement Collection (WEC) index data from over 215,000 students in Years 4–10 across South Australia (2016–2019) to examine the relationship between wellbeing and academic outcomes. Learning readiness emerged as the most influential wellbeing component, with a more pronounced influence on male students particularly in literacy; effects were stable across year levels and time periods. The findings demonstrate learning analytics' potential for deepening system-level understanding of socio-emotional factors' relationship to academic success. |
| 283 | [Learning Analytics in Schools: Is Digital Data Use Influenced by Teacher-Level or School-Level Factors?](https://learning-analytics.info/index.php/JLA/article/view/8567) | paper | Surveys 2,247 teachers across 112 Swiss upper secondary schools to investigate what teacher-level and school-level factors drive educators' use of digital data for instructional decisions. Teacher-specific attributes — technology confidence, positive attitudes toward digital tools, and data access — significantly predict digital data use, while school-level factors such as collegial collaboration and principal support operated indirectly through individual teacher characteristics. The authors suggest findings can guide customized professional development strategies aligned with educator strengths and supportive institutional conditions. |
| 284 | [Targeted School-Based Interventions for Improving Reading and Mathematics for Students with or at Risk of Academic Difficulties in Grades K–6](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/improving-reading-mathematics-academic-difficulties-grade-k-6) | report | This 2021 Campbell systematic review synthesized 205 high-quality studies (607 total) examining school-based interventions for students with or at risk of academic difficulties in grades K–6. Peer-assisted instruction and small-group adult instruction showed the largest improvements in both reading and mathematics. Effects were positive in the short term with some fadeout but detectable gains up to two years post-intervention. |
| 285 | [Targeted School-Based Interventions for Improving Reading and Mathematics for Students with or at Risk of Academic Difficulties in Grades 7–12](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/improving-reading-mathematics-academic-difficulties-grade-7-12) | report | This 2020 Campbell systematic review examined 71 studies (52 RCTs) of school-based interventions for at-risk students in grades 7–12. Average effects on standardized reading and math tests were positive and statistically significant, with mathematics interventions showing larger effects than reading. Small-group instruction substantially outperformed computer-assisted instruction. |
| 286 | [Mindfulness-Based Interventions for Improving Cognition, Academic Achievement, Behavior, and Socio-Emotional Functioning of Primary and Secondary Students](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/mindfulness-based-interventions-primary-and-secondary-school-students) | report | This 2017 Campbell systematic review synthesized 35 randomized or quasi-experimental studies (6,207 participants) of school-based mindfulness interventions. MBIs produced small, statistically significant positive effects on cognitive and socioemotional outcomes, but not on behavioral or academic outcomes. The high risk of bias across included studies means further high-quality evidence is needed. |
| 287 | [Effects of Trauma-Informed Approaches in Schools](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/effects-of-trauma-informed-approaches-in-schools) | report | This 2019 Campbell systematic review searched for studies of trauma-informed approaches in schools — programs integrating workforce development, trauma-focused services, and organizational environment changes — but found zero studies meeting inclusion criteria. Despite widespread adoption of trauma-informed practices in US and international schools, no rigorous evaluations had been conducted at the time of the review. |
| 288 | [21st Century Adaptive Teaching and Individualized Learning Operationalized as Specific Blends of Student-Centered Instructional Events](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/adaptive-teaching-individualized-learning-classroom-settings-k-12) | report | This 2019 Campbell systematic review analyzed 299 studies (43,175 students, 2000–2017) comparing student-centered versus teacher-centered instruction in K–12. More student-centered conditions produced moderate positive effects on achievement; however, giving students excessive control over pacing showed a significantly negative relationship with outcomes. The combination of adaptability with a guiding teacher role produced the strongest effects. |
| 289 | [The Relationship Between Homework Time and Academic Performance Among K-12: A Systematic Review](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/the-relationship-between-homework-time-and-academic-performance-among-k-12-a-systematic-review) | report | This 2024 Campbell systematic review examined 11 experimental studies on homework effects for K–12 students. Homework showed a beneficial effect on primary school academic achievement, especially arithmetic computation and problem-solving. However, only one of the included studies was published after 1995, and the optimal duration of homework remains unclear, pointing to an urgent need for contemporary high-quality experiments. |
| 290 | [Mobile Device Use in the Primary School Classroom and Impact on Pupil Literacy and Numeracy Attainment: A Systematic Review](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/mobile-device-use-in-the-primary-school-classroom-and-impact-on-pupil-literacy-and-numeracy-attainment-a-systematic-review) | report | This 2024 Campbell systematic review examined 18 studies (11,126 participants) of tablets, smartphones, and handheld digital games in primary classrooms for children aged 4–12. Children using mobile devices showed better results on maths and literacy tests compared to control conditions, though study quality was mostly moderate-to-high risk of bias. Insufficient evidence exists to determine which device types or teaching strategies are most effective. |
| 291 | [Montessori Education's Impact on Academic and Nonacademic Outcomes](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/montessori-impact-on-academic-and-nonacademic-outcomes) | report | This 2023 Campbell meta-analysis examined 32 rigorous studies (1970–2020) comparing Montessori to traditional education across preschool through high school in eight countries. Students in Montessori settings scored approximately 0.25 SD higher on academic outcomes and 0.33 SD higher on non-academic outcomes (executive function, well-being, social skills, creativity). Effects were strongest at preschool/elementary levels and in randomized studies. |
| 292 | [Small Class Sizes for Improving Student Achievement in Primary and Secondary Schools](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/small-class-sizes-student-achievement-primary-and-secondary-schools) | report | This 2018 Campbell systematic review examined 127 studies from 41 countries (10 in meta-analysis) on class size effects on academic achievement. The review concluded that small class sizes have at best a very small effect on reading and no significant effect on mathematics. Given the high cost of class size reduction and minimal demonstrated benefit, the authors emphasize the need for better evidence before resource allocation. |
| 293 | [Later School Start Times for Supporting the Education, Health, and Well-Being of High School Students](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/later-school-start-times-education-health-well-being-high-school-students) | report | This 2017 Campbell systematic review examined 17 reports of 11 interventions (297,994 participants across six countries) on delayed high school start times. Later start times appear to increase sleep, and some studies suggest positive associations with academic and psychosocial outcomes, but overall evidence quality is low and insufficient to draw firm conclusions. Possible adverse effects include reduced parent interaction and scheduling difficulties. |
| 294 | [The Effectiveness of Volunteer Tutoring Programs](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/effectiveness-of-volunteer-tutoring-programmes) | report | This 2006 Campbell systematic review analyzed 28 study cohorts (1,676 participants, grades K–8) of volunteer tutoring programs in the USA. Programs showed positive effects on reading and language outcomes equivalent to approximately one-third of a standard deviation improvement (overall reading effect size: d = 0.23–0.30). Highly structured tutoring programs produced significantly greater effects than less structured approaches; reading comprehension and mathematics showed positive but non-significant trends. |
| 295 | [Dropout Prevention and Intervention Programs: Effects on School Completion and Dropout Among School-Aged Children and Youth](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/dropout-prevention-intervention-programmes) | report | This 2011 Campbell systematic review examined 548 reports describing 167 studies (1985–2010) of school and community-based dropout prevention programs. The review found that school dropout can be reduced through carefully implemented programs, but no single prevention or intervention strategy stood out as better than any other. Implementation quality and program intensity mattered more than the specific program type chosen. |
| 296 | [Impacts of After-School Programs on Student Outcomes](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/impacts-of-after-school-programmes-on-student-outcomes) | report | This 2006 Campbell systematic review examined five experimental studies of after-school programs combining academics with youth development and recreation activities. Programs did not contribute to higher reading achievement (d = 0.028, ns) or significant grade improvements, though they reduced unsupervised self-care by more than 0.5 SD and increased participation in enriching activities. Eighty-four percent of measured impacts showed no significant differences between program and control youth. |
| 297 | [Approaches to Parent Involvement for Improving the Academic Performance of Elementary School-Aged Children](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/parent-involvement-improving-academic-performance-school-children) | report | This 2006 Campbell systematic review found that parent involvement has a positive and significant effect on children's overall academic performance (overall d = 0.45; direct parent involvement programs d = 0.31). Benefits were observed even with a median intervention duration of only 11 weeks. The review provides support for parent involvement as a viable supplementary intervention, backing policies like the No Child Left Behind parent involvement mandate. |
| 298 | [School-Based Interventions for Reducing Disciplinary School Exclusion](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/reducing-school-exclusion-school-based-interventions) | report | This 2018 Campbell systematic review examined 37 RCTs (mostly US and UK) of school-based interventions targeting exclusion rates in children aged 4–18. Interventions caused a small, significant drop in exclusion rates during the first six months, but effects did not sustain long-term, and no impact on antisocial behavior was detected. Academic skills enhancement, counselling, mentoring, and teacher skills training all showed some promise. |
| 302 | [Indicated Truancy Interventions: Effects on School Attendance Among Chronic Truant Students](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/truancy-interventions-effects-on-school-attendance) | report | This 2012 Campbell systematic review examined 28 studies from the US, Canada, UK, and Australia (1,725 participants; 16 in meta-analysis) of truancy interventions for chronically absent students. Interventions were effective: treated students attended an average of 4.7 more school days than controls. No significant differences in effectiveness were found across different delivery channels or program formats, though attendance remained below acceptable levels in most cases. |
| 303 | [The Effects of Teachers' Classroom Management Practices on Disruptive or Aggressive Student Behaviour](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/classroom-management-effects-on-disruptive-aggressive-behaviour) | report | This 2011 Campbell systematic review examined 12 studies of teacher classroom management practices in US and Dutch K–12 classrooms. Teachers' classroom management practices had a significant positive effect in decreasing aggressive or problematic student behavior across all included studies. However, the small sample sizes prevent definitive conclusions, and researchers could not identify which specific management components were most effective. |
| 304 | [Service Learning for Improving Academic Success in Students in Grades K to 12](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/service-learning-for-improving-academic-success-primary-and-secondary-students) | report | This 2022 Campbell systematic review examined 10 US-based studies (over 8,000 participants) of school-based service learning — programs linking community service to academic instruction — in grades K–12. Evidence regarding academic success was inconclusive due to insufficient comparable studies, and no adverse effects were documented. The review is constrained to US contexts, limiting international generalizability. |
| 305 | [Psychosocial Interventions for School Refusal with Primary and Secondary School Students](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/psychosocial-interventions-for-school-refusal) | report | This 2015 Campbell systematic review examined 8 rigorous studies (435 participants, 1980–2013) of psychosocial interventions for school refusal in primary and secondary students. Cognitive-behavioral therapy (CBT) significantly increased school attendance but had no statistically significant effect on anxiety. Findings should be treated with caution due to unclear randomization in most studies and the small evidence base. |
| 306 | ['No Excuses' Charter Schools for Increasing Math and Literacy Achievement in Primary and Secondary Education](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/charter-schools-maths-and-literacy-primary-and-secondary-education) | report | This 2017 Campbell systematic review examined 18 US studies (5 RCTs, 13 quasi-experimental, 1990–2015) of 'No Excuses' charter schools, which combine high academic expectations, strict discipline, extended instruction time, and intensive teacher training. These schools are associated with greater student gains on standardized math and literacy measures than traditional public schools, with notably stronger effects in mathematics that grew over three years. Caution is warranted due to reliance on studies from a single charter management organization (KIPP). |
| 307 | [Self-Management Interventions for Reducing Challenging Behaviors Among School-Age Students](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/self-management-interventions-school-student-behaviour) | report | This 2022 Campbell systematic review analyzed 75 single-case design studies and 4 group-design studies of school-based self-management interventions for students with challenging behaviors. Interventions showed moderate effects for both behavioral and academic outcomes — including on-task behavior, prosocial behaviors, and work completion — with more meaningful effects for African-American students and students receiving special education services. |
| 308 | [The Effect of Linguistic Comprehension Instruction on Generalized Language and Reading Comprehension Skills](https://www.campbellcollaboration.org/review/linguistic-training-effect-on-language-and-reading-comprehension) | report | This 2019 Campbell systematic review analyzed 43 studies (1992–2017) of linguistic comprehension interventions in preschool and school-aged children. Results showed a small positive immediate effect on generalized linguistic comprehension (vocabulary and grammar gains were small; narrative and listening comprehension gains were moderate), with negligible effects on reading comprehension. Programs appear insufficient to close vocabulary gaps, and preventing reading difficulties likely requires longer-term educational efforts. |
| 310 | [The complex ramifications of Students for Fair Admissions v. Harvard](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/the-complex-ramifications-of-students-for-fair-admissions-v-harvard/) | report | This analysis documents a "cascade effect" following the 2023 Supreme Court ruling that ended race-conscious college admissions: underrepresented minority students displaced from elite institutions move to state flagships and less-selective schools, reshaping enrollment patterns across higher education. Black student enrollment has declined most sharply at elite colleges, and standardized testing policy significantly mediates diversity outcomes across institution types. |
| 312 | [How immigration enforcement is harming US schools and students](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/how-immigration-enforcement-is-harming-us-schools-and-students/) | report | This article synthesizes research showing that intensified immigration enforcement correlates with increased chronic absenteeism, mental health deterioration, declining test scores, and reduced college enrollment rates among students from immigrant families, with particularly severe effects on Latino and English learner populations. The rescission of sanctuary protections for schools has left educators facing mounting pressures without adequate federal guidance or support systems. |
| 314 | [Parents are not fully aware of, or concerned about, their children's school attendance](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/parents-are-not-fully-aware-of-or-concerned-about-their-childrens-school-attendance/) | report | This research finds that fewer than half of caregivers whose children are at risk of chronic absenteeism report being concerned about it, and reveals a significant gap between actual attendance data and parent-reported absences. Many parents view available online learning as offsetting the importance of in-person attendance, despite expressing belief that classroom presence matters—an attitude that may undermine post-pandemic recovery efforts. |
| 316 | [Career and technical education is a hidden weak spot in many high schools' teacher workforces](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/career-and-technical-education-is-a-hidden-weak-spot-in-many-high-schools-teacher-workforces/) | report | Using Tennessee data, this research finds that CTE teachers in high-growth occupational fields—IT, health sciences, and STEM—leave teaching at significantly higher rates than other educators, with departing instructors earning approximately 20% more in private sector roles. This turnover creates staffing challenges that sometimes result in fewer course offerings for students in career and technical programs. |
| 320 | [Many high schools across the United States offer limited civics-focused extracurricular activities](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/many-high-schools-across-the-united-states-offer-limited-civics-focused-extracurricular-activities/) | report | Using a nationally representative survey of approximately 650 high school principals, this research finds that while 80% of schools offer student government, other civic activities—Model UN (19%), mock trials (26%)—remain rare. High-poverty schools provide substantially fewer civics opportunities than low-poverty schools, raising equity concerns about students' access to civic learning experiences that support democratic participation. |
| 321 | [Early reading tests can aid educational recovery after the pandemic](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/early-reading-tests-can-aid-educational-recovery-after-the-pandemic/) | report | This study presents Virginia data showing that over one-quarter of kindergarten and first-grade students fell significantly behind in early reading skills in fall 2020—a 10-percentage-point increase from the prior year. The authors emphasize evidence-based interventions aligned with the science of reading to address emerging disparities and prevent long-term academic struggles. |
| 322 | [School finance reforms made funding more equal by income, but not by race](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/school-finance-reforms-made-funding-more-equal-by-income-but-not-by-race/) | report | Analyzing state school finance reforms since 1990, this research finds that income-based reforms reduced funding disparities between low- and high-income districts by over $1,300 per pupil but "either did not change or widened funding gaps" between districts by racial composition, increasing racial funding inequalities by $740–$1,100 per pupil. The authors argue income-based policies alone cannot address racial disparities without considering school segregation patterns. |
| 323 | [2016 Brown Center Report on American Education: How Well Are American Students Learning?](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/2016-brown-center-report-on-american-education-how-well-are-american-students-learning/) | report | This flagship annual report covers three topics: Common Core implementation effects on math and reading instruction (finding no evidence that Common Core caused 2015 test score declines), whether eighth-grade math tracking correlates with AP success (states with more tracked classes produced more high-performing AP students across racial groups), and international variation in school principal leadership. The analysis concludes that high-performing countries show principals are reluctant to offer direct teaching advice. |
| 324 | [The impact of COVID-19 on student achievement and what it may mean for educators](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/the-impact-of-covid-19-on-student-achievement-and-what-it-may-mean-for-educators/) | report | This study projects pandemic learning losses using historical test score data from millions of students, estimating that students could return to school in fall 2020 with roughly 70% of their prior-year reading gains and less than 50% of math gains. The authors also project increased variability in academic achievement levels, warning that equity gaps will widen across the student population. |
| 325 | [The pandemic has had devastating impacts on learning. What will it take to help students catch up?](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/the-pandemic-has-had-devastating-impacts-on-learning-what-will-it-take-to-help-students-catch-up/) | report | Measuring fall 2021 test scores for grades 3–8, this research found average math scores were 0.20–0.27 standard deviations below pre-pandemic levels, with reading also declining significantly. The study evaluates whether common recovery interventions—tutoring, summer programs, class-size reductions—are sufficient in scale and evidence base to close pandemic-era learning gaps, noting that equity disparities widened considerably during school closures. |
| 326 | [A restorative approach to student discipline shows promise in reducing suspensions and arrests](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/a-restorative-approach-to-student-discipline-shows-promise-in-reducing-suspensions-and-arrests/) | report | Examining Chicago Public Schools' implementation of restorative practices, this research finds that the program reduced student suspension days by 18% in the four years after rollout, with Black students experiencing double the average reductions. The study provides causal evidence that restorative approaches—emphasizing dialogue and harm reparation over punishment—can meaningfully reduce disciplinary disparities. |
| 327 | [Reclassifying English Language Learners: What's the effect on Wisconsin high schoolers?](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/reclassifying-english-language-learners-whats-the-effect-on-wisconsin-high-schoolers/) | report | This study finds that reclassifying English Language Learners as fully proficient during high school increases composite ACT scores by about one full point and boosts postsecondary enrollment, particularly at four-year institutions. The results suggest schools should ensure equitable access to college preparation resources for students scoring near proficiency reclassification thresholds. |
| 328 | [Do teacher expectations matter?](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/do-teacher-expectations-matter/) | report | Analyzing nationally representative data from approximately 6,000 tenth-grade students, this research provides causal evidence that teacher expectations directly affect students' long-term educational attainment. The authors also document that white teachers hold systematically lower expectations for Black students compared to Black teachers, and that this racial bias in expectations has measurable negative effects on Black students' college completion rates. |
| 329 | [How does student-teacher matching affect suspensions for students of color?](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/how-does-student-teacher-matching-affect-suspensions-for-students-of-color/) | report | Using 10 years of New York City public school data, this study finds that Black, Latino, and Asian American students experience fewer suspensions when assigned to teachers of the same racial or ethnic background. The authors estimate that increasing teacher diversity could prevent thousands of student suspensions annually and keep students in classrooms for thousands more instructional days over a decade. |
| 330 | [High school computer science impacts college majors and increases earnings](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/high-school-computer-science-impacts-colleg-majors-and-increases-earnings/) | report | Using Maryland longitudinal data, this research finds that taking a high school computer science course leads to a five-percentage-point increase in students' likelihood of earning a bachelor's degree in CS and improves employment outcomes. Benefits are particularly pronounced for historically underrepresented groups, though these same students show lower enrollment rates when CS courses become available—pointing to access and encouragement gaps. |
| 332 | [Special education: Beneficial to many, harmful to others](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/special-education-beneficial-to-some-harmful-to-others/) | report | Examining a 2005 Texas policy capping special education enrollment, this research finds that students who genuinely need special education services benefit significantly from them, while those inappropriately placed experience worse long-term educational outcomes. Notably, reducing racial disproportionality in placement improved outcomes for Black students, suggesting some were previously misclassified into special education rather than supported through general education. |
| 333 | [Class size: What research says and what it means for state policy](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/class-size-what-research-says-and-what-it-means-for-state-policy/) | report | This review of empirical literature finds that reductions of 7–10 fewer students per class can produce meaningful academic gains, particularly for disadvantaged elementary students, but that the magnitude of effect varies considerably across studies and grade levels. The authors conclude that policymakers must weigh class-size reduction against other evidence-based educational investments given the substantial and ongoing fiscal costs involved. |
| 334 | [Examining Implementation and Outcomes of the Project On-Track High-Dosage Tutoring Program](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/examining-implementation-and-outcomes-project-track-high-dosage-tutoring-program) | report | REL Appalachia (REL 2024-005) examined how northeastern Tennessee school districts implemented the Niswonger Foundation's Project On-Track literacy tutoring initiative for early elementary students. The program uses Amplify Reading to group students by skill level and generate mini-lessons aligned to the science of reading. After one year, 42% of students who started as most at-risk improved to lower risk categories; rural schools heavily favored in-school delivery (92%) compared to non-rural schools (47%). |
| 335 | [Effectiveness of Early Literacy Instruction: Summary of 20 Years of Research](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/systematic-literature-review/effectiveness-early-literacy-instruction-summary-20-years-research) | report | REL Southeast (REL 2021-084) synthesized over 74,000 studies published between 1997 and 2017, ultimately identifying 132 evidence-based early literacy interventions across 109 high-quality experimental and quasi-experimental studies. The review found that domain-specific instruction reliably increases performance in that domain, and that combined phonological awareness and print knowledge instruction shows promise for improving decoding and early writing skills. Language-focused instruction was found to be more effective in small group or one-on-one settings. |
| 336 | [Developing a Measure of Middle School Math Teachers' Pedagogical Content Knowledge for Computational Thinking](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/developing-measure-middle-school-math-teachers-pedagogical-content-knowledge-computational-thinking) | report | REL Midwest (REL 2026-007) developed and validated the CT-PCK Survey, a brief instrument measuring middle school teachers' ability to integrate computational thinking into mathematics instruction. Teachers scored approximately 61% correct on average, with those holding traditional certifications and those with five or more years of experience performing significantly higher. The survey is designed for use in professional development evaluation and institutional needs assessment in Midwest urban schools facing declining math achievement. |
| 337 | [A Pilot Study of Write to Succeed: An Intervention to Enhancing Writing Instruction in New Mexico](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/pilot-study-write-succeed-intervention-enhancing-writing-instruction-new-mexico) | report | REL Southwest (REL 2025-013) evaluated the Write to Succeed professional learning program in partnership with the New Mexico Public Education Department. The authors recommend that California investigate which regionalized special education programs benefit students in small districts and gather additional data to support more complex analyses of outcomes attributable to specific SELPA configurations. |
| 339 | [Implementation of Career- and College-Ready Requirements for High School Graduation in Washington](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/implementation-career-and-college-ready-requirements-high-school-graduation-washington) | report | REL Northwest (REL 2020-020) examined Washington state's 2014 legislation requiring 24 credits for high school graduation, finding that district implementation grew from 9% for the class of 2018 to 56% for the class of 2019. Only 27% of 2018 graduates statewide met all requirements, with significant gaps among economically disadvantaged students, English learners, and students of color. | 340 | [Additional Certification for Teachers in New York State: Teachers' Experience and Employment Location, Certification Pathways, and Certification Areas](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/additional-certification-teachers-new-york-state-teachers-experience-and-employment-location) | report | REL Northeast & Islands (REL 2022-110) studied how New York State teachers with at least one year of experience obtained additional certificates between October 2015 and October 2017. | 342 | [Stabilizing School Performance Indicators in New Jersey to Reduce the Effect of Random Error](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/stabilizing-school-performance-indicators-new-jersey-reduce-effect-random-error) | report | REL Mid-Atlantic (REL 2025-009) tested Bayesian hierarchical modeling as a method to reduce the impact of random error on school accountability indicators in New Jersey, particularly for smaller student groups. | 350 | [Exploring Implementation of Trauma-Engaged Practices in Alaska Schools](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/exploring-implementation-trauma-engaged-practices-alaska-schools) | report | REL Northwest (REL 2025-011) examined how Alaska schools implement trauma-engaged practices using the state's Transforming Schools Framework, drawing on administrative data and surveys. | 351 | [Applying a Cycle of Evidence-Based Continuous Improvement When Selecting Interventions and Project Components to Improve Attendance](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/resource/fact-sheetinfographicfaq/applying-cycle-evidence-based-continuous-improvement-when-selecting-interventions-project) | framework | REL West published this December 2023 fact sheet presenting an overview and worked example of how districts can apply a continuous improvement cycle when selecting attendance interventions or project components to reduce chronic absence. | 352 | [Principal Retention Patterns in Arizona, Nevada, and Utah](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/principal-retention-patterns-arizona-nevada-and-utah) | report | REL West (REL 2021-129) examined principal retention across Arizona, Nevada, and Utah, finding that fewer than half of principals in each state remained at the same school over the four-year period from fall 2016 to fall 2020. Retention rates varied significantly by school performance level, with substantially lower retention at lower-performing schools. When principals left their positions, those who remained in the profession tended to transfer within the same school district rather than to a different district. |
| 353 | [Student Group Differences in Arkansas' Indicators of Postsecondary Readiness and Success](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/student-group-differences-arkansas-indicators-postsecondary-readiness-and-success) | report | REL Southwest (REL 2023-145) analyzed middle and high school indicators predicting postsecondary success in Arkansas using data from grade 6 cohorts from 2008/09–2009/10, examining variation by race/ethnicity, economic status, disability status, and other characteristics. Indicator attainment varied substantially across student groups, with the largest differences between students with and without a designated disability; academic indicators showed greater disparities than behavioral ones. Predictive accuracy of indicators remained largely consistent across demographic groups, though specific indicators — notably ELA and science proficiency in middle school — showed variation in predictive strength for some populations. |
| 354 | [English Language Development Among American Indian English Learner Students in New Mexico](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/english-language-development-american-indian-english-learner-students-new-mexico) | report | REL Southwest (REL 2022-135) tracked two cohorts of American Indian English learner students who entered kindergarten in 2013/14 or 2014/15 in New Mexico public schools, examining English proficiency progress over five years. The majority were not reclassified as English proficient within the state's ESSA-aligned five-year goal timeframe, and most did not meet grade-level standards in ELA and math in grades 3 and 4. Students attending a bilingual multicultural education program for at least four years showed higher reclassification and academic achievement rates than peers without such exposure. |
| 356 | [Exploring Implementation of Attendance Supports to Reduce Chronic Absenteeism in the Providence Public School District](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/report/descriptive-study/exploring-implementation-attendance-supports-reduce-chronic-absenteeism-providence-public-school) | report | REL Northeast & Islands (REL 2021-099) examined attendance support initiatives in Providence Public School District during 2018–19, identifying which supports were most effective at reducing chronic absenteeism. Schools experiencing decreased chronic absenteeism implemented text messaging, phone calls, and mentorship programs with greater fidelity; multilingual text outreach showed particular promise in reaching families. Schools with declining absenteeism rates adopted attendance-related text messaging faster than schools where absenteeism increased. |
| 357 | [Ask an Expert: College- and Career-Readiness Interventions](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/resource/other-resource/ask-expert-college-and-career-readiness-interventions) | report | REL Appalachia (published June 2022) compiled this resource identifying 15 college and career readiness interventions that meet ESSA Tier 1, 2, or 3 evidence standards, with particular attention to whether research was conducted in rural areas or with economically disadvantaged students. | 358 | [High-Quality Early Child Education is Associated With Outcomes](https://ies.ed.gov/use-work/resource-library/resource/fact-sheetinfographicfaq/high-quality-early-child-education-associated) | report | REL West published this November 2025 infographic summarizing research-identified outcomes associated with high-quality early childhood education across four domains: kindergarten readiness, educational progress, long-term outcomes including college enrollment and employment, and health benefits. The summary identifies four essential features of high-quality programs: evidence-based curricula with play-based instruction and teacher coaching; educators with bachelor's degrees and K–12 equivalent compensation; small class sizes with low staff-to-child ratios; and full-day formats. Disadvantaged populations — including low-income children and children of color — were found to benefit most substantially. |

| 389 | [Common Core of Data (CCD)](https://nces.ed.gov/ccd/aboutCCD.asp) | dataset | The Common Core of Data (CCD) is the Department of Education's primary database on public elementary and secondary education in the United States, comprising a comprehensive annual national database of all public elementary and secondary schools and school districts. | 391 | [High School Longitudinal Study of 2009 (HSLS:09)](https://nces.ed.gov/surveys/hsls09/) | dataset | HSLS:09 is a nationally representative longitudinal study of more than 23,000 9th graders from 944 schools in 2009, with follow-ups in 2012 and 2016 and postsecondary administrative records through 2021. Data collection waves span from the 2009 base year through postsecondary transcripts, enabling research on transitions from high school into college and the workforce, including 10 state-representative datasets. |
| 392 | [Education Longitudinal Study of 2002 (ELS:2002)](https://nces.ed.gov/surveys/els2002/) | dataset | ELS:2002 is a nationally representative longitudinal study of 10th graders in 2002, following students through secondary and postsecondary years with data collection waves spanning from the 2002 base year through a 2012 third follow-up and 2013 postsecondary transcripts. The study focuses on students' trajectories from high school into postsecondary education, the workforce, and beyond, including patterns of college access and persistence, with surveys of students, parents, math and English teachers, and school administrators. High school transcripts and postsecondary transcripts are available for research on coursetaking and grades; public-use data are accessible through the NCES DataLab. |
| 393 | [National Education Longitudinal Study of 1988 (NELS:88)](https://nces.ed.gov/surveys/nels88/) | dataset | NELS:88 is a nationally representative longitudinal study of 8th graders in 1988, following students through secondary and postsecondary years across four follow-ups through 2000, 12 years after the baseline survey. The study collected surveys from students on school, work, and home experiences, educational aspirations, and peer and parent influences, along with student assessments in reading, social studies, mathematics, and science at 8th, 10th, and 12th grades. | 395 | [Civil Rights Data Collection (CRDC)](https://civilrightsdata.ed.gov/about) | dataset | The Civil Rights Data Collection (CRDC) has collected civil rights data related to students' access and barriers to educational opportunity from early childhood through grade 12 since 1968, and since the 2011–12 collection has been administered every two years to all public school districts and schools in the 50 states and Washington, D.C. The CRDC collects information on student enrollment, access to courses, programs, and school staff, and school climate factors such as bullying, harassment, and student discipline, with most data disaggregated by race, ethnicity, sex, disability, and English Learner status. It is a key enforcement tool under civil rights laws prohibiting discrimination based on race, color, national origin, sex, disability, and age. |

| 398 | [PISA — Programme for International Student Assessment (Data)](https://www.oecd.org/pisa/data/) | dataset | The PISA database contains the full set of responses from individual students, school principals, and parents, available for download in SPSS and SAS formats across assessment cycles from 2000 to 2022. | 404 | [Stanford Education Data Archive (SEDA)](https://cepa.stanford.edu/seda/overview) | dataset | The Stanford Education Data Archive, produced by the Educational Opportunity Project at Stanford University, is the first national database of academic performance measuring educational opportunity in every community in America across reading and math. The archive supports three active projects — the 2009-2019 Educational Opportunity Project (updated February 2026 with new trend data), the Education Recovery Project tracking learning loss and pandemic recovery across districts, and the Segregation Tracking Project mapping school and neighborhood segregation over decades. | 405 | [PSLC DataShop KDD Cup 2010 Educational Data Mining Challenge](https://pslcdatashop.web.cmu.edu/KDDCup/) | dataset | The KDD Cup 2010 challenge asked participants to predict student performance on mathematical problems from logs of student interaction with Intelligent Tutoring Systems, using five datasets (three development, two challenge) drawn from real ITS interactions. The task explored how generally or narrowly students learn, how quickly they improve, and whether knowledge requirements of problems can be inferred directly from student performance data. | 408 | [ASSISTments Dataset](https://sites.google.com/site/assistmentsdata/) | dataset | The ASSISTments data repository hosts multiple publicly released datasets from the ASSISTments intelligent tutoring platform, covering student interactions, skill builder sequences, affect data, and log files from various research studies published from 2009 onward.
